style="background:#fff3f3;"><b>40.53%</b> <i>886,804</i></td>\n<td style="background:#f0f0ff;">30.93% <i>676,794</i></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#f0f0ff;"><a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1964" title="United States presidential election, 1964">1964</a></td>\n<td style="background:#fff3f3;">48.85% <i>905,941</i></td>\n<td style="background:#f0f0ff;"><b>51.15%</b> <i>948,540</i></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#fff3f3;"><a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1960" title="United States presidential election, 1960">1960</a></td>\n<td style="background:#fff3f3;"><b>51.51%</b> <i>795,476</i></td>\n<td style="background:#f0f0ff;">48.49% <i>748,700</i></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p>In 1998, Democratic voters dominated areas of the state with a high percentage of racial minorities and transplanted white liberals from the northeastern United States, known colloquially as "snowbirds".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_New_York_Times_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_York_Times-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/South_Florida" title="South Florida">South Florida</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Miami_metropolitan_area" title="Miami metropolitan area">Miami metropolitan area</a> are dominated by both racial minorities and white liberals. Because of this, the area has consistently voted as one of the most Democratic areas of the state. The Daytona Beach area is similar demographically and the city of Orlando has a large Hispanic population, which has often favored Democrats. Republicans, made up mostly of white conservatives, have dominated throughout much of the rest of Florida, particularly in the more rural and suburban areas. This is characteristic of its voter base throughout the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_New_York_Times_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_York_Times-139">[139]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The fast-growing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I-4_corridor" title="I-4 corridor">I-4 corridor</a> area, which runs through <a href="/wiki/Central_Florida" title="Central Florida">Central Florida</a> and connects the cities of <a href="/wiki/Daytona_Beach,_Florida" title="Daytona Beach, Florida">Daytona Beach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tampa" title="Tampa">Tampa</a>/<a href="/wiki/St._Petersburg,_Florida" title="St. Petersburg, Florida">St. Petersburg</a>, has had a fairly even breakdown of Republican and Democratic voters. The area is often seen as a merging point of the conservative northern portion of the state and the liberal southern portion, making it the biggest swing area in the state. Since the late 20th century, the voting results in this area, containing 40% of Florida voters, has often determined who will win the state of Florida in presidential elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Washington_Times_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Washington_Times-140">[140]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The Democratic Party has maintained an edge in voter registration, both statewide and in 40 of the 67 counties, including <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County,_Florida" title="Miami-Dade County, Florida">Miami-Dade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Broward_County,_Florida" title="Broward County, Florida">Broward</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Palm_Beach_County,_Florida" title="Palm Beach County, Florida">Palm Beach</a> counties, the state\'s three most populous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voter_Registration_by_Party_Affiliation_and_County_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voter_Registration_by_Party_Affiliation_and_County-141">[141]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Elections_of_2000_to_present">Elections of 2000 to present</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida,_2000" title="United States presidential election in Florida, 2000">United States presidential election in Florida, 2000</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:307px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida,_2016.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3201" data-file-width="2904" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida%2C_2016.svg/305px-United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida%2C_2016.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida%2C_2016.svg/458px-United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida%2C_2016.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida%2C_2016.svg/610px-United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida%2C_2016.svg.png 2x" width="305"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida,_2016.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treemap" title="Treemap">Treemap</a> of the popular vote by county, 2016 presidential election.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In 2000, <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> won the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2000" title="United States presidential election, 2000">U.S. Presidential election</a> by a margin of 271\xe2\x80\x93266 in the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archives2000_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-archives2000-142">[142]</a></sup> Of the 271 electoral votes for Bush, 25 were cast by electors from Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archives1_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-archives1-143">[143]</a></sup> The Florida results were contested and a recount was ordered by the court, with the results settled in a court decision.</p>\n<p>Reapportionment following the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">2010 United States Census</a> gave the state two more seats in the House of Representatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CENSUS_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-CENSUS-144">[144]</a></sup> The legislature\'s redistricting, announced in 2012, was quickly challenged in court, on the grounds that it had unfairly benefited Republican interests. In 2015, the Florida Supreme Court ruled on appeal that the congressional districts had to be redrawn because of the legislature\'s violation of the Fair District Amendments to the state constitution passed in 2010; it accepted a new map in early December 2015.</p>\n<p>The political make-up of congressional and legislative districts has enabled Republicans to control the governorship and most statewide elective offices, and 17 of the state\'s 27 seats in the 2012 <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections_in_Florida,_2012" title="United States House of Representatives elections in Florida, 2012">House of Representatives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Florida has been listed as a <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">swing state</a> in Presidential elections since 1950, voting for the losing candidate once in that period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Florida1_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Florida1-146">[146]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In the closely contested <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2000,_in_Florida" title="United States presidential election, 2000, in Florida">2000 election</a>, the state played a pivotal role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archives2000_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-archives2000-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archives1_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-archives1-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Study_of_Disputed_Florida_Ballots_Finds_Justices_Did_Not_Cast_the_Deciding_Vote_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Study_of_Disputed_Florida_Ballots_Finds_Justices_Did_Not_Cast_the_Deciding_Vote-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Out of more than 5.8 million votes for the two main contenders Bush and <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a>, around 500 votes separated the two candidates for the all-decisive Florida electoral votes that landed Bush the election win. Florida\'s <a href="/wiki/Felony_disenfranchisement" title="Felony disenfranchisement">felony disenfranchisement</a> law is more severe than most European nations or other American states. A 2002 study in the <i><a href="/wiki/American_Sociological_Review" title="American Sociological Review">American Sociological Review</a></i> concluded that "if the state\'s 827,000 disenfranchised felons had voted at the same rate as other Floridians, Democratic candidate Al Gore would have won Florida\xe2\x80\x94and the presidency\xe2\x80\x94by more than 80,000 votes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-restore_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-restore-151">[151]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2008, delegates of both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)_presidential_primaries,_2008#GOP_February_5_rule" title="Republican Party (United States) presidential primaries, 2008">Republican Florida primary election</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)_presidential_primaries,_2008#Nullified_primaries" title="Democratic Party (United States) presidential primaries, 2008">Democratic Florida primary election</a> were stripped of half of their votes when the conventions met in August due to violation of both parties\' national rules.</p>\n<p>In the 2010 elections, Republicans solidified their dominance statewide, by winning the governor\'s mansion, and maintaining firm majorities in both houses of the state legislature. They won four previously Democratic-held seats to create a 19\xe2\x80\x936 Republican-majority delegation representing Florida in the federal House of Representatives.</p>\n<p>In 2010, more than 63% of state voters approved the initiated Amendments 5 and 6 to the state constitution, to ensure more fairness in districting. These have become known as the Fair District Amendments. As a result of the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">2010 United States Census</a>, Florida gained two House of Representative seats in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CENSUS_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-CENSUS-144">[144]</a></sup> The legislature issued revised congressional districts in 2012, which were immediately challenged in court by supporters of the above amendments.</p>\n<p>The court ruled in 2014, after lengthy testimony, that at least two districts had to be redrawn because of gerrymandering. After this was appealed, in July 2015 the Florida Supreme Court ruled that lawmakers had followed an illegal and unconstitutional process overly influenced by party operatives, and ruled that at least eight districts had to be redrawn. On December 2, 2015, a 5\xe2\x80\x932 majority of the Court accepted a new map of congressional districts, some of which was drawn by challengers. Their ruling affirmed the map previously approved by <a href="/wiki/Leon_County,_Florida" title="Leon County, Florida">Leon County</a> Judge Terry Lewis, who had overseen the original trial. It particularly makes changes in South Florida. There are likely to be additional challenges to the map and districts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>\n<p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Sentencing_Project" title="The Sentencing Project">The Sentencing Project</a>, the effect of Florida\'s felony disenfranchisement law is such that in 2014, "[m]ore than one in ten Floridians \xe2\x80\x93 and nearly one in four African-American Floridians \xe2\x80\x93 are [were] shut out of the polls because of felony convictions," although they had completed sentences and parole/probation requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statutes">Statutes</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Florida" title="Law of Florida">Law of Florida</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FlaSupremeCrtBldgFeb08.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1528" data-file-width="2755" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/FlaSupremeCrtBldgFeb08.JPG/220px-FlaSupremeCrtBldgFeb08.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/FlaSupremeCrtBldgFeb08.JPG/330px-FlaSupremeCrtBldgFeb08.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/FlaSupremeCrtBldgFeb08.JPG/440px-FlaSupremeCrtBldgFeb08.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FlaSupremeCrtBldgFeb08.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florida_Supreme_Court" title="Florida Supreme Court">Florida Supreme Court</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The state repealed mandatory auto inspection in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_laws_include_auto_inspection_repeal_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_laws_include_auto_inspection_repeal-154">[154]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 1972, the state made <a href="/wiki/Personal_injury_protection" title="Personal injury protection">personal injury protection</a> auto insurance mandatory for drivers, becoming the second in the nation to enact a <a href="/wiki/No-fault_insurance" title="No-fault insurance">no-fault insurance</a> law. The ease of receiving payments under this law is seen as precipitating a major increase in insurance fraud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Auto insurance fraud was the highest in the nation in 2011, estimated at close to $1 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> Fraud is particularly centered in the Miami-Dade metropolitan and Tampa areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_enforcement">Law enforcement</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_law_enforcement_agencies_in_Florida" title="List of law enforcement agencies in Florida">List of law enforcement agencies in Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crime_in_Florida" title="Crime in Florida">Crime in Florida</a></div>\n<p>Florida was ranked the fifth-most dangerous state in 2009. Ranking was based on the record of serious felonies committed in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> The state was the sixth highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scam" title="Scam">scammed</a> state in 2010. It ranked first in mortgage fraud in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2009, 44% of highway fatalities involved alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft100207_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft100207-162">[162]</a></sup> Florida is one of seven states that prohibit the <a href="/wiki/Open_carry_in_the_United_States" title="Open carry in the United States">open carry of handguns</a>. This law was passed in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>\n<p>According to the Federal Trade Commission, Florida has the highest per capita rate of both reported fraud and other types of complaints including identity theft complaints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012_Consumer_Sentinel_Network_Data_Book_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-2012_Consumer_Sentinel_Network_Data_Book-164">[164]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_GDP" title="List of U.S. states by GDP">List of U.S. states by GDP</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2528" data-file-width="3000" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching.jpg/250px-Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching.jpg/375px-Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching.jpg/500px-Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nLaunch of <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia"><i>Columbia</i></a> from the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Florida.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="882" data-file-width="759" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Florida.png/220px-Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Florida.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Florida.png/330px-Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Florida.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Florida.png/440px-Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Florida.png 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Florida.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMap of Florida showing average income by county.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brickell1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Brickell1.JPG/250px-Brickell1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Brickell1.JPG/375px-Brickell1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Brickell1.JPG/500px-Brickell1.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brickell1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brickell_Financial_District" title="Brickell Financial District">Brickell Financial District</a> in Miami contains the largest concentration of international banks in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In the twentieth century, tourism, industry, construction, international banking, biomedical and life sciences, healthcare research, simulation training, aerospace and defense, and commercial space travel have contributed to the state\'s economic development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>\n<p>The Gross Domestic Product (GDP) of Florida in 2016 was $926 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Its GDP is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_GDP" title="List of U.S. states by GDP">fourth largest</a> economy in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gross_Domestic_Product_by_state_Table_8:Gross_Domestic_Product_by_State_in_Current_Dollars.2C_2003.E2.80.932006_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gross_Domestic_Product_by_state_Table_8:Gross_Domestic_Product_by_State_in_Current_Dollars.2C_2003.E2.80.932006-168">[168]</a></sup> In 2010, it became the fourth largest exporter of trade goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> The major contributors to the state\'s gross output in 2007 were general services, financial services, trade, transportation and public utilities, manufacturing and construction respectively. In 2010\xe2\x80\x9311, the state budget was $70.5 billion, having reached a high of $73.8 billion in 2006\xe2\x80\x9307.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Chief Executive Magazine name Florida the third "Best State for Business" in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The economy is driven almost entirely by its nineteen metropolitan areas. In 2004, they had a combined total of 95.7% of the state\'s domestic product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_income">Personal income</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florida_locations_by_per_capita_income" title="Florida locations by per capita income">Florida locations by per capita income</a></div>\n<p>In 2011, Florida\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_GDP_per_capita_(nominal)" title="List of U.S. states by GDP per capita (nominal)">per capita personal income</a> was $39,563, ranking 27th in the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STATE_PERSONAL_INCOME_2011_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-STATE_PERSONAL_INCOME_2011-173">[173]</a></sup> In February 2011, the state\'s unemployment rate was 11.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Florida is one of seven states that do not impose a personal <a href="/wiki/Income_tax" title="Income tax">income tax</a>.</p>\n<p>Florida\'s constitution establishes a state <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a> that is adjusted for inflation annually. As of January 1, 2017, Florida\'s minimum wage was $5.08 for <i>tipped positions</i>, and $8.10 for <i>non-tipped</i> positions, which was higher than the federal rate of $7.25.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minwage_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-minwage-175">[175]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Florida has 4 cities in the top 25 cities in the U.S. with the most credit card debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Top_Cities_With_Credit_Card_Debt_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Top_Cities_With_Credit_Card_Debt-176">[176]</a></sup> The state also had the second-highest credit card delinquency rate, with 1.45% of cardholders in the state more than 90 days delinquent on one or more credit cards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TBFP_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-TBFP-177">[177]</a></sup></p>\n<p>There were 2.4 million Floridians living in poverty in 2008. 18.4% of children 18 and younger were living in poverty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poverty_estimates_pain_sad_picture_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poverty_estimates_pain_sad_picture-178">[178]</a></sup> Miami is the sixth poorest big city in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> In 2010, over 2.5 million Floridians were on food stamps, up from 1.2 million in 2007. To qualify, Floridians must make less than 133% of the federal poverty level, which would be under $29,000 for a family of four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2.5_million_on_Fla._food_stamps_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-2.5_million_on_Fla._food_stamps-180">[180]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Real_estate">Real estate</span></h3>\n<p>In the early 20th century, land speculators discovered Florida, and businessmen such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_Plant" title="Henry Plant">Henry Plant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Flagler" title="Henry Flagler">Henry Flagler</a> developed railroad systems, which led people to move in, drawn by the weather and local economies. From then on, tourism boomed, fueling a cycle of development that overwhelmed a great deal of farmland.</p>\n<p>Due to the huge payouts by the insurance industry as a result of the hurricane claims of 2004, homeowners insurance has risen 40% to 60% and deductibles have risen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft091231_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-ft091231-61">[61]</a></sup></p>\n<p>At the end of the third quarter in 2008, Florida had the highest mortgage delinquency rate in the U.S., with 7.8% of mortgages delinquent at least 60 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TBFP_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-TBFP-177">[177]</a></sup> A 2009 list of national housing markets that were hard hit in the real estate crash included a disproportionate number in Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-America.27s_25_Weakest_Housing_Markets_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-America.27s_25_Weakest_Housing_Markets-181">[181]</a></sup> The early 21st-century building boom left Florida with 300,000 vacant homes in 2009, according to state figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Our_views:Playing_with_fire_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Our_views:Playing_with_fire-182">[182]</a></sup> In 2009, the US Census Bureau estimated that Floridians spent an average 49.1% of personal income on housing-related costs, the third highest percentage in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Census_Bureau:_1_in_3_Virginians_Pays_Plenty_for_Housing_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Census_Bureau:_1_in_3_Virginians_Pays_Plenty_for_Housing-183">[183]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In the third quarter of 2009, there were 278,189 delinquent loans, 80,327 foreclosures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-No_to_noncourt_foreclosures_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-No_to_noncourt_foreclosures-184">[184]</a></sup> Sales of existing homes for February 2010 was 11,890, up 21% from the same month in 2009. Only two metropolitan areas showed a decrease in homes sold: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panama_City_%E2%80%93_Lynn_Haven_%E2%80%93_Panama_City_Beach,_Florida_Metropolitan_Statistical_Area" title="Panama City \xe2\x80\x93 Lynn Haven \xe2\x80\x93 Panama City Beach, Florida Metropolitan Statistical Area">Panama City</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palm_Bay_%E2%80%93_Melbourne_%E2%80%93_Titusville,_Florida_Metropolitan_Statistical_Area" title="Palm Bay \xe2\x80\x93 Melbourne \xe2\x80\x93 Titusville, Florida Metropolitan Statistical Area">Brevard County</a>. The average sales price for an existing house was $131,000, 7% decrease from the prior year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brevard_home_sales_down_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brevard_home_sales_down-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (March 2011)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">\xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Talk:Florida#Paid_article_title_indicates_a_discussion_of_one_county_only.2C_not_entire_state" title="Talk:Florida">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Port_of_Miami_20071208.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="632" data-file-width="2243" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Port_of_Miami_20071208.jpg/280px-Port_of_Miami_20071208.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Port_of_Miami_20071208.jpg/420px-Port_of_Miami_20071208.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Port_of_Miami_20071208.jpg/560px-Port_of_Miami_20071208.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Port_of_Miami_20071208.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_of_Miami" title="Port of Miami">Port of Miami</a> is the world\'s largest cruise ship port.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magic_Kingdom_castle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1545" data-file-width="1262" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Magic_Kingdom_castle.jpg/170px-Magic_Kingdom_castle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Magic_Kingdom_castle.jpg/255px-Magic_Kingdom_castle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Magic_Kingdom_castle.jpg/340px-Magic_Kingdom_castle.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magic_Kingdom_castle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_World_Resort" title="Walt Disney World Resort">Walt Disney World Resort</a> in Orlando.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>If you can\'t find something to do in Florida, you\'re just boring...</p>\n<div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x89<cite><a href="/wiki/Guy_Fieri" title="Guy Fieri">Guy Fieri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celebrity_chef" title="Celebrity chef">celebrity chef</a>, 2017<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Tourism makes up one of the largest sectors of the state economy, with nearly 1.4 million people employed in the tourism industry in 2016 (a record for the state, surpassing the 1.2 million employment from 2015).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-187">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flgov.com_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-flgov.com-188">[188]</a></sup> In 2015, Florida broke the 100-million visitor mark for the first time in state history by hosting a record 105 million visitors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flgov.com_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-flgov.com-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> and broke that record in 2016 with 112.8 million tourists; Florida has set tourism records for six consecutive years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-187">[187]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Many beach towns are popular tourist destinations, particularly during winter and <a href="/wiki/Spring_break" title="Spring break">spring break</a>. Twenty-three million tourists visited Florida beaches in 2000, spending $22 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beaches_get_pumped_up_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beaches_get_pumped_up-190">[190]</a></sup> The public has a right to beach access under the <a href="/wiki/Public_trust_doctrine" title="Public trust doctrine">public trust doctrine</a>, but some areas have access effectively blocked by private owners for a long distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Amusement_park" title="Amusement park">Amusement parks</a>, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Orlando" title="Greater Orlando">Greater Orlando</a> area, make up a significant portion of tourism. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_World_Resort" title="Walt Disney World Resort">Walt Disney World Resort</a> is the most visited vacation resort in the world with over 50 million annual visitors, consisting of four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theme_park" title="Theme park">theme parks</a>, 27 themed <a href="/wiki/Resort" title="Resort">resort hotels</a>, 9 non\xe2\x80\x93Disney hotels, two <a href="/wiki/Water_park" title="Water park">water parks</a>, four <a href="/wiki/Golf_course" title="Golf course">golf courses</a> and other recreational venues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> Other major theme parks in the area include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Orlando_Resort" title="Universal Orlando Resort">Universal Orlando Resort</a>, <a href="/wiki/SeaWorld_Orlando" title="SeaWorld Orlando">SeaWorld Orlando</a> and <a href="/wiki/Busch_Gardens_Tampa" title="Busch Gardens Tampa">Busch Gardens Tampa</a>.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture_and_fishing">Agriculture and fishing</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IFAS_citrus02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="479" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/IFAS_citrus02.jpg/180px-IFAS_citrus02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/IFAS_citrus02.jpg/270px-IFAS_citrus02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/IFAS_citrus02.jpg/360px-IFAS_citrus02.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IFAS_citrus02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nFlorida oranges</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Agriculture is the second largest industry in the state. Citrus fruit, especially oranges, are a major part of the economy, and Florida produces the majority of citrus fruit grown in the United States. In 2006, 67% of all citrus, 74% of oranges, 58% of tangerines, and 54% of grapefruit were grown in Florida. About 95% of commercial orange production in the state is destined for processing (mostly as orange juice, the official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_beverage" title="State beverage">state beverage</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Commodity_Profile:_Citrus_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Commodity_Profile:_Citrus-193">[193]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Citrus_canker" title="Citrus canker">Citrus canker</a> continues to be an issue of concern. From 1997 to 2013, the growing of citrus trees has declined 25%, from 600,000 acres (240,000\xc2\xa0ha) to 450,000 acres (180,000\xc2\xa0ha). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citrus_greening" title="Citrus greening">Citrus greening</a> disease is incurable. A study states that it has caused the loss of $4.5 billion between 2006 and 2012. As of 2014, it was the major agricultural concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Other products include sugarcane, strawberries, tomatoes and celery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crop_Profile_for_Celery_in_Florida_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crop_Profile_for_Celery_in_Florida-195">[195]</a></sup> The state is the largest producer of <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">sweet corn</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_beans" title="Green beans">green beans</a> for the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The Everglades Agricultural Area is a major center for agriculture. The environmental impact of agriculture, especially <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, is a major issue in Florida today.</p>\n<p>In 2009, fishing was a $6 billion industry, employing 60,000 jobs for sports and commercial purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Locals_to_protest_fish_regulation_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Locals_to_protest_fish_regulation-197">[197]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miami_Civic_Center_20100619.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1810" data-file-width="3294" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Miami_Civic_Center_20100619.jpg/250px-Miami_Civic_Center_20100619.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Miami_Civic_Center_20100619.jpg/375px-Miami_Civic_Center_20100619.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Miami_Civic_Center_20100619.jpg/500px-Miami_Civic_Center_20100619.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miami_Civic_Center_20100619.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civic_Center_(Miami)" title="Civic Center (Miami)">Miami Civic Center</a> has the second-largest concentration of medical and research facilities in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Florida is the leading state for sales of <a href="/wiki/Motorboat" title="Motorboat">powerboats</a>. Boats sales totaled $1.96 billion in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fl150210_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-fl150210-199">[199]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">Mining</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Phosphate#Occurrence" title="Phosphate">Phosphate mining</a>, concentrated in the <a href="/wiki/Bone_Valley" title="Bone Valley">Bone Valley</a>, is the state\'s third-largest industry. The state produces about 75% of the phosphate required by farmers in the United States and 25% of the world supply, with about 95% used for agriculture (90% for fertilizer and 5% for livestock feed supplements) and 5% used for other products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-About_Phosphate_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-About_Phosphate-200">[200]</a></sup></p>\n<p>After the watershed events of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Andrew" title="Hurricane Andrew">Hurricane Andrew</a> in 1992, the state of Florida began investing in economic development through the Office of Trade, Tourism, and Economic Development. Governor <a href="/wiki/Jeb_Bush" title="Jeb Bush">Jeb Bush</a> realized that watershed events such as Andrew negatively impacted Florida\'s backbone industry of tourism severely. The office was directed to target Medical/Bio-Sciences among others. Three years later, The <a href="/wiki/Scripps_Research_Institute" title="Scripps Research Institute">Scripps Research Institute</a> (TSRI) announced it had chosen Florida for its newest expansion. In 2003, TSRI announced plans to establish a major science center in Palm Beach, a 364,000 square feet (33,800\xc2\xa0m<sup>2</sup>) facility on 100 acres (40\xc2\xa0ha), which TSRI planned to occupy in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h3>\n<p>Since the development of the federal NASA <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Merritt_Island_launch_sites" title="List of Merritt Island launch sites">Merritt Island launch sites</a> on Cape Canaveral (most notably Kennedy Space Center) in 1962, Florida has developed a sizable <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace industry</a>.</p>\n<p>Another major economic engine in Florida is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_military" title="United States military">United States military</a>. There are 24 military bases in the state, housing three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unified_Combatant_Command" title="Unified Combatant Command">Unified Combatant Commands</a>; <a href="/wiki/United_States_Central_Command" title="United States Central Command">United States Central Command</a> in Tampa, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Southern_Command" title="United States Southern Command">United States Southern Command</a> in <a href="/wiki/Doral,_Florida" title="Doral, Florida">Doral</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Special_Operations_Command" title="United States Special Operations Command">United States Special Operations Command</a> in Tampa. Some 109,390 U.S. military personnel stationed in Florida,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> contributing, directly and indirectly, $52 billion a year to the state\'s economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Military-friendly_bill_cruise_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Military-friendly_bill_cruise-203">[203]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2009, there were 89,706 federal workers employed within the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> Tens of thousands more employees work for contractors who have federal contracts, including those with the military.</p>\n<p>In 2012, government of all levels was a top employer in all counties in the state, because this classification includes public school teachers and other school staff. School boards employ nearly 1 of every 30 workers in the state. The federal military was the top employer in three counties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h2>\n<p>There were 2.7 million <a href="/wiki/Medicaid" title="Medicaid">Medicaid</a> patients in Florida in 2009. The governor has proposed adding $2.6 billion to care for the expected 300,000 additional patients in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-County_Medicaid_tab_rises.2C_could_get_worse_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-County_Medicaid_tab_rises.2C_could_get_worse-206">[206]</a></sup> The cost of caring for 2.3 million clients in 2010 was $18.8 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> This is nearly 30% of Florida\'s budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Medicaid paid for 60% of all births in Florida in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watchdog_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watchdog-62">[62]</a></sup> The state has a <a href="/wiki/Florida_Medicaid_waiver" title="Florida Medicaid waiver">program</a> for those not covered by Medicaid.</p>\n<p>In 2013, Florida refused to participate in providing coverage for the uninsured under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affordable_Care_Act" title="Affordable Care Act">Affordable Care Act</a>, popularly called Obamacare. The Florida legislature also refused to accept additional Federal funding for Medicaid, although this would have helped its constituents at no cost to the state. As a result, Florida is second only to Texas in the percentage of its citizens without health insurance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SouthBeachMiamiBeach.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/SouthBeachMiamiBeach.jpg/250px-SouthBeachMiamiBeach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/SouthBeachMiamiBeach.jpg/375px-SouthBeachMiamiBeach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/SouthBeachMiamiBeach.jpg/500px-SouthBeachMiamiBeach.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SouthBeachMiamiBeach.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Miami_Beach_Architectural_District" title="Miami Beach Architectural District">Miami Art Deco District</a>, built during the 1920s\xe2\x80\x931930s.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Florida has the largest collection of <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Streamline_Moderne" title="Streamline Moderne">Streamline Moderne</a> buildings in both the United States and the entire world, most of which are located in the <a href="/wiki/Miami_metropolitan_area" title="Miami metropolitan area">Miami metropolitan area</a>, especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami_Beach" title="Miami Beach">Miami Beach</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Miami_Beach_Architectural_District" title="Miami Beach Architectural District">Art Deco District</a>, constructed as the city was becoming a resort destination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> A unique architectural design found only in Florida is the post-World War II <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami_Modern_Architecture" title="Miami Modern Architecture">Miami Modern</a>, which can be seen in areas such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami,_Florida" title="Miami, Florida">Miami</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MiMo_Historic_District" title="MiMo Historic District">MiMo Historic District</a>.</p>\n<p>Being of early importance as a regional center of banking and finance, the <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Jacksonville" title="Architecture of Jacksonville">architecture of Jacksonville</a> displays a wide variety of styles and design principles. Many of state\'s earliest skyscrapers were constructed in Jacksonville, dating as far back as 1902,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metrojacksonville_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metrojacksonville-211">[211]</a></sup> and last holding a state height record from 1974 to 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emp_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-emp-212">[212]</a></sup> The city is endowed with one of the largest collections of <a href="/wiki/Prairie_School" title="Prairie School">Prairie School</a> buildings outside of the Midwest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pst_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-pst-213">[213]</a></sup> Jacksonville is also noteworthy for its collection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-Century_modern" title="Mid-Century modern">Mid-Century modern</a> architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jhs_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jhs-214">[214]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Some sections of the state feature architectural styles including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Colonial_Revival_Style_architecture" title="Spanish Colonial Revival Style architecture">Spanish revival</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida_cracker_architecture" title="Florida cracker architecture">Florida vernacular</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Revival_Style" title="Mediterranean Revival Style">Mediterranean Revival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Official:_Design_rules_haven.27t_cost_Palm_Bay_new_businesses_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Official:_Design_rules_haven.27t_cost_Palm_Bay_new_businesses-216">[216]</a></sup> A notable collection of these styles can be found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Augustine" title="St. Augustine">St. Augustine</a>, the oldest continuously occupied European-established settlement within the borders of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhl_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-nhl-217">[217]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">\n<tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" media">Category:Florida media</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Florida" title="Education in Florida">Education in Florida</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_and_secondary_education">Primary and secondary education</span></h3>\n<p>Florida\'s primary and secondary school systems are administered by the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Department_of_Education" title="Florida Department of Education">Florida Department of Education</a>. School districts are organized within county boundaries. Each school district has an elected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Board_of_Education" title="Board of Education">Board of Education</a> that sets policy, budget, goals, and approves expenditures. Management is the responsibility of a <a href="/wiki/Superintendent_(education)" title="Superintendent (education)">Superintendent of schools</a>.</p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Florida_Department_of_Education" title="Florida Department of Education">Florida Department of Education</a> is required by law to train educators in teaching <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_language_learning_and_teaching" title="English language learning and teaching">English for Speakers of Other Languages</a> (ESOL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-consentdecree_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-consentdecree-218">[218]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Universities">Universities</span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/State_University_System_of_Florida" title="State University System of Florida">State University System of Florida</a> was founded in 1905, and is governed by the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Board_of_Governors" title="Florida Board of Governors">Florida Board of Governors</a>. During the 2010 academic year, 312,216 students attended one of these twelve universities. The <a href="/wiki/Florida_College_System" title="Florida College System">Florida College System</a> comprises 28 public community and state colleges. In 2011\xe2\x80\x9312, enrollment consisted of more than 875,000 students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> As of 2017 the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Central_Florida" title="University of Central Florida">University of Central Florida</a>, with over 64,000 students, is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_university_campuses_by_enrollment" title="List of United States university campuses by enrollment">largest university by enrollment</a> in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Florida\'s first private university, <a href="/wiki/Stetson_University" title="Stetson University">Stetson University</a>, was founded in 1883. The <a href="/wiki/Independent_Colleges_and_Universities_of_Florida" title="Independent Colleges and Universities of Florida">Independent Colleges and Universities of Florida</a> is an association of 28 private, educational institutions in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> This Association reported that their member institutions served over 121,000 students in the fall of 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005.E2.80.932006_Accountability_Report:_Quality.2C_Productivity.2C_Diversity.2C_and_Access_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-2005.E2.80.932006_Accountability_Report:_Quality.2C_Productivity.2C_Diversity.2C_and_Access-222">[222]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2016, Florida charged the second lowest tuition in the nation for four years, $26,000 for in-state students, to $86,000 for out-of-state students. This compares with an average of 34,800 nationally for in-state students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 180px">\n<div style="width: 180px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 178px;">\n<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" Florida">Orlando</a></small></p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_Florida" title="Transportation in Florida">Transportation in Florida</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yeehaw_Junction_Turnpike_east01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Yeehaw_Junction_Turnpike_east01.jpg/220px-Yeehaw_Junction_Turnpike_east01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Yeehaw_Junction_Turnpike_east01.jpg/330px-Yeehaw_Junction_Turnpike_east01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Yeehaw_Junction_Turnpike_east01.jpg/440px-Yeehaw_Junction_Turnpike_east01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yeehaw_Junction_Turnpike_east01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Florida%27s_Turnpike" title="Florida\'s Turnpike">Florida\'s Turnpike</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Highways">Highways</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_Roads_in_Florida" title="State Roads in Florida">State Roads in Florida</a></div>\n<p>Florida\'s highway system contains 1,473\xc2\xa0mi (2,371\xc2\xa0km) of interstate highway, and 9,934\xc2\xa0mi (15,987\xc2\xa0km) of non-interstate highway, such as state highways and U.S. Highways. Florida\'s <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">interstates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Highway_System" title="Florida State Highway System">state highways</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Highways" title="U.S. Highways">U.S. Highways</a> are maintained by the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Department_of_Transportation" title="Florida Department of Transportation">Florida Department of Transportation</a>.</p>\n<p>In 2011, there were about 9,000 <a href="/wiki/Filling_station" title="Filling station">retail gas stations</a> in the state. Floridians consume 21 million gallons of gasoline daily, ranking it third in national use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> Motorists have the 45th lowest rate of car insurance in the U.S. 24% are uninsured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Drivers between 15 and 19 years of age averaged 364 car crashes a year per ten thousand licensed Florida drivers in 2010. Drivers 70 and older averaged 95 per 10,000 during the same time frame. A spokesperson for the non-profit <a href="/wiki/Insurance_Institute_for_Highway_Safety" title="Insurance Institute for Highway Safety">Insurance Institute</a> said that "Older drivers are more of a threat to themselves."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Before the construction of routes under the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aid_Highway_Act_of_1956" title="Federal Aid Highway Act of 1956">Federal Aid Highway Act of 1956</a>, Florida began construction of a long cross-state <a href="/wiki/Toll_road" title="Toll road">toll road</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida%27s_Turnpike" title="Florida\'s Turnpike">Florida\'s Turnpike</a>. The first section, from <a href="/wiki/Fort_Pierce,_Florida" title="Fort Pierce, Florida">Fort Pierce</a> south to the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Glades_Interchange" title="Golden Glades Interchange">Golden Glades Interchange</a> was completed in 1957. After a second section north through Orlando to <a href="/wiki/Wildwood,_Florida" title="Wildwood, Florida">Wildwood</a> (near present-day <a href="/wiki/The_Villages,_Florida" title="The Villages, Florida">The Villages</a>), and a <a href="/wiki/Homestead_Extension_of_Florida%27s_Turnpike" title="Homestead Extension of Florida\'s Turnpike">southward extension</a> around Miami to <a href="/wiki/Homestead,_Florida" title="Homestead, Florida">Homestead</a>, it was finished in 1974.</p>\n<p>Florida\'s primary interstate routes include:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><img alt="" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="601" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/I-4.svg/20px-I-4.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/I-4.svg/30px-I-4.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/I-4.svg/40px-I-4.svg.png 2x" width="20"/> <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Interstate_4" title="Interstate 4">I\xe2\x80\x914</a></span>, which spans 133 miles, bisects the state, connecting <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lakeland,_Florida" title="Lakeland, Florida">Lakeland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Daytona_Beach,_Florida" title="Daytona Beach, Florida">Daytona Beach</a>, connecting with <a href="/wiki/Interstate_75_in_Florida" title="Interstate 75 in Florida">I-75</a> in Tampa and I-95 in Daytona Beach.</li>\n<li><img alt="" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="601" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/I-10.svg/20px-I-10.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/I-10.svg/30px-I-10.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/I-10.svg/40px-I-10.svg.png 2x" width="20"/> <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Interstate_10_in_Florida" title="Interstate 10 in Florida">I-10</a></span>, which spans 362 miles in Florida, traverses the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Panhandle" title="Florida Panhandle">panhandle</a>, connecting <a href="/wiki/Pensacola,_Florida" title="Pensacola, Florida">Pensacola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tallahassee,_Florida" title="Tallahassee, Florida">Tallahassee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_City,_Florida" title="Lake City, Florida">Lake City</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville</a>, with interchanges with I-75 in Lake City and I-95 in Jacksonville. It is the southernmost interstate in the United States terminating in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santa_Monica" title="Santa Monica">Santa Monica</a> with a total length of 2460 miles.</li>\n<li><img alt="" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="601" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/I-75.svg/20px-I-75.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/I-75.svg/30px-I-75.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/I-75.svg/40px-I-75.svg.png 2x" width="20"/> <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Interstate_75_in_Florida" title="Interstate 75 in Florida">I-75</a></span>, which spans 470 miles in Florida, enters the state near Lake City (45 miles (72\xc2\xa0km) west of <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville</a>) and continues southward through <a href="/wiki/Gainesville,_Florida" title="Gainesville, Florida">Gainesville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocala,_Florida" title="Ocala, Florida">Ocala</a>, Tampa\'s eastern suburbs, <a href="/wiki/Bradenton,_Florida" title="Bradenton, Florida">Bradenton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarasota,_Florida" title="Sarasota, Florida">Sarasota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fort_Myers,_Florida" title="Fort Myers, Florida">Fort Myers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naples,_Florida" title="Naples, Florida">Naples</a>, where it crosses the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alligator_Alley" title="Alligator Alley">Alligator Alley</a>" as a <a href="/wiki/Toll_road" title="Toll road">toll road</a> to <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale,_Florida" title="Fort Lauderdale, Florida">Fort Lauderdale</a> before turning southward and terminating in <a href="/wiki/Hialeah,_Florida" title="Hialeah, Florida">Hialeah</a>/<a href="/wiki/Miami_Lakes,_Florida" title="Miami Lakes, Florida">Miami Lakes</a> having interchanges with I-10 in Lake City and I-4 in Tampa.It is the second longest north south interstate with a total length of 1786 miles and terminates at the Canadian border at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sault_Ste._Marie,_MI" title="Sault Ste. Marie, MI">Sault Ste. Marie, MI</a>.</li>\n<li><img alt="" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="601" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/I-95.svg/20px-I-95.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/I-95.svg/30px-I-95.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/I-95.svg/40px-I-95.svg.png 2x" width="20"/> <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Interstate_95_in_Florida" title="Interstate 95 in Florida">I-95</a></span>, which spans 382 miles in Florida, enters the state near Jacksonville and continues along the Atlantic Coast through Daytona Beach, the <a href="/wiki/Space_Coast" title="Space Coast">Melbourne/Titusville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palm_Bay,_Florida" title="Palm Bay, Florida">Palm Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vero_Beach,_Florida" title="Vero Beach, Florida">Vero Beach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fort_Pierce,_Florida" title="Fort Pierce, Florida">Fort Pierce</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_Saint_Lucie,_Florida" title="Port Saint Lucie, Florida">Port Saint Lucie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stuart,_Florida" title="Stuart, Florida">Stuart</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Palm_Beach,_Florida" title="West Palm Beach, Florida">West Palm Beach</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale,_Florida" title="Fort Lauderdale, Florida">Fort Lauderdale</a>, before terminating in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downtown_Miami" title="Downtown Miami">Downtown Miami</a>, with interchanges with I-10 in Jacksonville and I-4 in Daytona Beach. There are four auxiliary routes associated with the interstate. It is the longest north south interstate with a total length of 1924 miles and terminates at the Canadian border northeast of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houlton,_ME" title="Houlton, ME">Houlton, ME</a>.</li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Airports">Airports</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Florida" title="List of airports in Florida">List of airports in Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aviation_in_Florida" title="Aviation in Florida">Aviation in Florida</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1_-_A%C3%A9roport_de_Miami_-_Ao%C3%BBt_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3471" data-file-width="7295" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/1_-_A%C3%A9roport_de_Miami_-_Ao%C3%BBt_2008.jpg/220px-1_-_A%C3%A9roport_de_Miami_-_Ao%C3%BBt_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/1_-_A%C3%A9roport_de_Miami_-_Ao%C3%BBt_2008.jpg/330px-1_-_A%C3%A9roport_de_Miami_-_Ao%C3%BBt_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/1_-_A%C3%A9roport_de_Miami_-_Ao%C3%BBt_2008.jpg/440px-1_-_A%C3%A9roport_de_Miami_-_Ao%C3%BBt_2008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1_-_A%C3%A9roport_de_Miami_-_Ao%C3%BBt_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Miami_International_Airport" title="Miami International Airport">Miami International Airport</a> is the world\'s 10th-busiest cargo airport, and second busiest airport for international passengers in the U.S.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Florida has 131 public airports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NDIC_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-NDIC-228">[228]</a></sup> Florida\'s seven large hub and medium hub airports, as classified by the FAA, are the following:</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tr valign="baseline">\n<th style="white-space:nowrap; text-align:left;">City align="right">2,755,719</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intercity_rail">Intercity rail</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:West_Palm_Beach_SAL_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/West_Palm_Beach_SAL_001.jpg/200px-West_Palm_Beach_SAL_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/West_Palm_Beach_SAL_001.jpg/300px-West_Palm_Beach_SAL_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/West_Palm_Beach_SAL_001.jpg/400px-West_Palm_Beach_SAL_001.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:West_Palm_Beach_SAL_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Amtrak" title="Amtrak">Amtrak</a> serves most major cities in Florida. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Palm_Beach_(Tri-Rail_station)" title="West Palm Beach (Tri-Rail station)">West Palm Beach Station</a> serves Amtrak and <a href="/wiki/Tri-Rail" title="Tri-Rail">Tri-Rail</a> <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail" title="Commuter rail">commuter rail</a> service.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Florida is served by <a href="/wiki/Amtrak" title="Amtrak">Amtrak</a>, operating numerous lines throughout, connecting the state\'s largest cities to points north in the United States and Canada. The busiest Amtrak train stations in Florida in 2011 were: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanford_(Amtrak_station)" title="Sanford (Amtrak station)">Sanford</a> (259,944), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orlando_(Amtrak_station)" title="Orlando (Amtrak station)">Orlando</a> (179,142), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Tampa,_Florida)" title="Union Station (Tampa, Florida)">Tampa Union Station</a> (140,785), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami_(Amtrak_station)" title="Miami (Amtrak station)">Miami</a> (94,556), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacksonville_(Amtrak_station)" title="Jacksonville (Amtrak station)">Jacksonville</a> (74,733).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sanford,_Florida" title="Sanford, Florida">Sanford</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Greater_Orlando" title="Greater Orlando">Greater Orlando</a>, is the southern terminus of the <a href="/wiki/Auto_Train" title="Auto Train">Auto Train</a>, which originates at <a href="/wiki/Lorton,_Virginia" title="Lorton, Virginia">Lorton, Virginia</a>, south of Washington, D.C. Until 2005, Orlando was also the eastern terminus of the <a href="/wiki/Sunset_Limited" title="Sunset Limited">Sunset Limited</a>, which travels across the southern United States via <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a> to its western terminus of Los Angeles. Florida is served by two additional Amtrak trains (the <a href="/wiki/Silver_Star_(Amtrak_train)" title="Silver Star (Amtrak train)">Silver Star</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Silver_Meteor" title="Silver Meteor">Silver Meteor</a>), which operate between New York City and Miami. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami_Central_Station" title="Miami Central Station">Miami Central Station</a>, the city\'s <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">rapid transit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail" title="Commuter rail">commuter rail</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intercity_rail" title="Intercity rail">intercity rail</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bus" title="Bus">bus</a> hub, is under construction.</p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Florida_Department_of_Transportation" title="Florida Department of Transportation">Florida Department of Transportation</a> was preparing to build a <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed rail</a> between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tampa" title="Tampa">Tampa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lakeland,_Florida" title="Lakeland, Florida">Lakeland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakeland_Brief_Mar_2010_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakeland_Brief_Mar_2010-230">[230]</a></sup> This was to be the first phase of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florida_High_Speed_Rail" title="Florida High Speed Rail">Florida High Speed Rail</a> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Environmental_Studies_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-Environmental_Studies-231">[231]</a></sup> Soil work began in July 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bullet_Train_Work_Begins_With_Soil_in_I-4_Median_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bullet_Train_Work_Begins_With_Soil_in_I-4_Median-232">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> and construction of the line was slated to begin in 2011, with the initial Tampa-Orlando phase completed by 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TBO_Payment_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-TBO_Payment-234">[234]</a></sup> The second phase, would have extended the line to Miami. Governor Scott, however, refused federal funds and the project has been canceled.</p>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All_Aboard_Florida" title="All Aboard Florida">All Aboard Florida</a> is a proposed higher-speed rail service that would run between <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miami_Beach,_Florida" title="Miami Beach, Florida">Miami</a> at speeds up to 125\xc2\xa0mph. Its Miami to <a href="/wiki/Cocoa,_Florida" title="Cocoa, Florida">Cocoa</a> portion is scheduled to open in 2016, with the final segment to Orlando opening in 2017.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_transit">Public transit</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metrorail-Tri-Rail.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Metrorail-Tri-Rail.png/200px-Metrorail-Tri-Rail.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Metrorail-Tri-Rail.png/300px-Metrorail-Tri-Rail.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Metrorail-Tri-Rail.png/400px-Metrorail-Tri-Rail.png 2x" width="200"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metrorail-Tri-Rail.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami_Metrorail" title="Miami Metrorail">Miami Metrorail</a> is the state\'s only <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">rapid transit</a> system. About 15% of Miamians use public transit daily.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_South_Florida" title="Transportation in South Florida">Transportation in South Florida</a></div>\n<ul>\n<li>Miami: Miami\'s public transportation is served by <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_Transit" title="Miami-Dade Transit">Miami-Dade Transit</a> that runs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami_Metrorail" title="Miami Metrorail">Metrorail</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_rail" title="Heavy rail">heavy rail</a> <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">rapid transit</a> system, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_Metromover" title="Miami-Dade Metromover">Metromover</a>, a <a href="/wiki/People_mover" title="People mover">people mover</a> train system in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downtown_Miami" title="Downtown Miami">Downtown Miami</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_Transit#Metrobus" title="Miami-Dade Transit">Metrobus</a>, Miami\'s bus system. Metrorail runs throughout <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County" title="Miami-Dade County">Miami-Dade County</a> and has two lines and 23 stations connecting to Downtown Miami\'s Metromover and <a href="/wiki/Tri-Rail" title="Tri-Rail">Tri-Rail</a>. Metromover has three lines and 21 stations throughout <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downtown_Miami" title="Downtown Miami">Downtown Miami</a>. Outside of Miami-Dade County, public transit in the <a href="/wiki/Miami_metropolitan_area" title="Miami metropolitan area">Miami metropolitan area</a> is served by <a href="/wiki/Broward_County_Transit" title="Broward County Transit">Broward County Transit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palm_Tran" title="Palm Tran">Palm Tran</a>; intercounty <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail" title="Commuter rail">commuter rail</a> service is provided by <a href="/wiki/Tri-Rail" title="Tri-Rail">Tri-Rail</a>, with 18 stations including the region\'s three international airports.</li>\n<li>Orlando: Orlando is served by the <a href="/wiki/SunRail" title="SunRail">SunRail</a> commuter train, which runs on a 32 miles (51\xc2\xa0km) (61 miles (98\xc2\xa0km) when complete) line including four stops in downtown. <a href="/wiki/Lynx_(Orlando)" title="Lynx (Orlando)">Lynx bus</a> serves the greater Orlando area in <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_Florida" title="Orange County, Florida">Orange</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seminole_County,_Florida" title="Seminole County, Florida">Seminole</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Osceola_County,_Florida" title="Osceola County, Florida">Osceola</a> counties.</li>\n<li>Tampa: Tampa and its surrounding area use the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hillsborough_Area_Regional_Transit_Authority" title="Hillsborough Area Regional Transit Authority">Hillsborough Area Regional Transit Authority</a> system ("HART"). In addition, downtown Tampa has continuous trolley services in the form of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heritage_trolley" title="Heritage trolley">heritage trolley</a> powered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tampa_Electric_Company" title="Tampa Electric Company">Tampa Electric Company</a>. Pinellas County and St. Petersburg provide similar services through the <a href="/wiki/Pinellas_Suncoast_Transit_Authority" title="Pinellas Suncoast Transit Authority">Pinellas Suncoast Transit Authority</a> or "PSTA". The beaches of <a href="/wiki/Pinellas_County,_Florida" title="Pinellas County, Florida">Pinellas County</a> also have a continuous trolley bus. Downtown St. Petersburg has a trolley system.</li>\n<li>Jacksonville: Jacksonville is served by the <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Skyway" title="Jacksonville Skyway">Jacksonville Skyway</a>, an automated people mover <a href="/wiki/Monorail" title="Monorail">monorail</a> connecting the <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_College_at_Jacksonville" title="Florida State College at Jacksonville">Florida State College</a> downtown campus, the Northbank central business district, Convention Center, and Southbank locations. The system includes 8 stops connected by two lines. <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Transportation_Authority" title="Jacksonville Transportation Authority">JTA</a> bus has 180 vehicles with 56 lines.</li>\n</ul>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<caption>Largest public transit systems in Florida (2012)</caption>\n<tr>\n<th class="unsortable">Rank</th>\n<th
